mathiasbynens: but since there's no <window> element, <body> acts as a convenience proxy for them

[14:12:05.0000] <Hixie_>
setting body.onload or <body onload> is equivalent to setting window.onload

[14:12:06.0000] <mathiasbynens>
ah, that explains it â€“ thanks Hixie_!

[14:12:27.0000] <Hixie_>
note that this means you can't catch an event 'load' that bubbles through a <body> element with an onload handler

[14:13:01.0000] <Hixie_>
which leads to funny results when your markup is <html onload="t(event)"> <body onload="t(event)"> <div onload="t(event)"> and you fire a bubbling 'load' event at the div

[14:13:53.0000] <mathiasbynens>
hah

[13:34:18.0000] <zenparsing>

[13:34:29.0000] <zenparsing>

[13:34:35.0000] <zenparsing>
an "asynchronous program error" occurs when a user entry point returns a promise which rejects

[13:34:46.0000] <zenparsing>

[13:34:58.0000] <zenparsing>

[15:04:29.0000] <Hixie_>
man, desigining promise-based apis with this whole "exceptions result in rejection" thing is annoying

[15:04:43.0000] <Hixie_>
it just doesn't map to the existing model at all

[15:05:05.0000] <Hixie_>
like, consider XHR

[15:05:15.0000] <Hixie_>
you really want rejection to mean "the result was not 2xx"

[15:05:39.0000] <Hixie_>
but now it also has to mean "send() was called when the state was not OPENED"

[15:05:46.0000] <Hixie_>
since it's anathema to have send() throw, apparently

[15:06:19.0000] <Hixie_>
even though it's pointless for that to be in a promise rejection since you know it synchronously and aren't ever going to try to catch it

[15:07:32.0000] <TabAtkins>
I thought that we weren't promisifying xhr, we were replacing it with fetch(), which doesn't have the same state-management issues.

[15:08:23.0000] <Hixie_>
xhr's just an example, the same problem occurs with lots of things

[15:08:31.0000] <Hixie_>
e.g. requestAutocomplete

[15:08:45.0000] <Hixie_>
rejection can be for any of the reasons that autocompleteerror currently fires

[15:08:54.0000] <TabAtkins>
Yeah.

[15:08:55.0000] <Hixie_>
but also for all the reasons that requestAutocomplete() currently throws

[15:09:02.0000] <TabAtkins>
Sure.

[15:09:09.0000] <Hixie_>
which are currently separate for good language design reasons

[15:09:11.0000] <Hixie_>
but with promises...

[15:09:57.0000] <Hixie_>
and actually fetch() does have the same problem. it doesn't reject if there was a network error.

[15:10:43.0000] <TabAtkins>
Handling two kinds of errors with sync throwing and async error events have the same usability problems the promise people are complaining about.

[15:11:06.0000] <Hixie_>
no it doesn't, because you never need to worry about the sync exceptions

[15:11:14.0000] <Hixie_>
nobody ever tries to catch them

[15:11:30.0000] <Hixie_>
and if they occur, your code just ends, it doesn't start running random code with invalid input

[15:11:33.0000] <TabAtkins>
Then don't worry about them.

[15:11:40.0000] <Hixie_>
but with rejection, you have to!

[15:11:53.0000] <Hixie_>
because now your code expecting a string rejection reason might get an exception rejection reason instead

[15:11:56.0000] <Hixie_>
or whatever

[15:12:59.0000] <TabAtkins>
You'll just throw or something when you end up trying to operate on the weird error anyway.

[15:13:18.0000] <Hixie_>
not necessarily

[15:13:30.0000] <Hixie_>
(mostly that's a result of JS lacking type checking)

[15:13:43.0000] <Hixie_>
(which is another language bug imho :-) )

[15:13:56.0000] <TabAtkins>
Agreed.

[15:17:52.0000] <jgraham>
I think Hixie has a point; if you think that using exceptions for flow control is bad and that using rejections for flow control is good (which seems to be part of the promise model), then using rejections to replace exceptions is bad

[15:18:23.0000] <Hixie_>
oh, wait, fetch() does reject the promise for network error

[15:18:36.0000] <Hixie_>
so fetch() has _exactly_ the problem i described for xhr

[15:19:39.0000] <Hixie_>
if you call fetch({ method: 'get', url: '/' }).then(a, b); where b() is some function to handle errors, you'll appear to always get a network error

[15:19:44.0000] <Hixie_>
but actually, you're not getting a network error

[15:19:56.0000] <Hixie_>
you're getting a type error because the arguments were type checked as bad

[15:20:11.0000] <Hixie_>
and there's no way i can see to distinguish those two completely unrelated cases

[15:21:24.0000] <Hixie_>
even if there was, e.g. because fetch() rejected with NetworkError instead of TypeError, you'd still be SOL. (a) because you wouldn't be able to get the network error information (e.g. status code, which XHR gives you), and (b) because you would never think to check for TypeError, so you'd assume it was a NetworkError and would _still_ think you always had a bed network

[15:21:34.0000] <Hixie_>
Domenic: ^ that's why you're wrong.

[15:22:58.0000] <Hixie_>
(the correct was to call it, for those who are wondering, is  fetch(new Request({ method: 'get', url: '/' })).then(a, b);  )

[15:24:03.0000] <Hixie_>
no wait, it's fetch(new Request('/', { method: 'get' })).then(a, b);

[15:24:11.0000] <TabAtkins>
How'd you get any network error information if it failed at the type-checking stage?

[15:24:29.0000] <Hixie_>
fetch() rejects with TypeError for a network error

[15:24:46.0000] <Hixie_>
and IDL rejects any promise-returning method with TypeError for an argument type error

[15:25:04.0000] <Hixie_>
either way, you get b() called with TypeError

[23:42:40.0000] <mathiasbynens>
Hixie: `includeSubdomains` makes sure that once the user visits https://whatwg.org/, *.whatwg.org is in the HSTS cache, and so entering `http://somesubdomain.whatwg.org/` or even `http://idontexist.whatwg.org/` in the address bar would go to HTTPS instantly without ever making a connection over HTTP

[23:43:44.0000] <mathiasbynens>
thatâ€™s important, because as soon as you hit HTTP a MitM attacker can just strip the redirect to HTTPS, or send a response that they control (e.g. phishing page)

[23:44:43.0000] <annevk>
mathiasbynens: how cna they hijack idontexist.whatwg.org?

[23:47:03.0000] <mathiasbynens>
annevk: if `includeSubdomains` is not set, and the user enters the non-HTTPS URL

[23:47:38.0000] <mathiasbynens>
or another (attacker-controlled) page includes a resource from that domain or links/redirects to it over HTTP etc.

[23:51:15.0000] <mathiasbynens>
with `includeSubdomains`, as soon as the user visits any page on `whatwg.org`, HSTS kicks in for the entire domain including subdomains, and all future requests will go over HTTPS (even for subdomains that havenâ€™t been visited before or that donâ€™t resolve)

[23:52:48.0000] <mathiasbynens>
to get to the same level of safety when `includeSubdomains` is not set, the user would have to visit all the existing *.whatwg.org domains, including those that donâ€™t even exist (â†’ âˆž), assuming all subdomains send HSTS headers as well

[00:12:37.0000] <annevk>

[00:18:46.0000] <mathiasbynens>

[06:45:19.0000] <jgraham>
SteveF_: FWIW I think that, although longdesc hasn't been mentioned here, it is the constant elephant in the room for a11y discussions. The discussion reached a point where it was clear to me, at least, that if I go blind, anything that required authors to use longdesc would simply not be accessible to me, whether or not it is in the HTML spec. At that point a valid tactic on the "WHATWG side" (if you want that kind of unhelpful characterisation) wo

[06:45:27.0000] <jgraham>
... think this attribute is entirely useless if not outright harmful, but it's not worth fighting about". Obviously that didn't happen, and perhaps it was a mistake. On the other side proponents of the attribute showed very little sign that they were engaging with the evidence that it wasn't really a helpful design. Instead they sunk a lot of political capital â€” more than they could really afford â€” on forcing it through. Several of the participa

[06:45:33.0000] <jgraham>
... had toxic personalities and people weren't prepared to cut them out of the discussion, or disown their behaviour, particularly when they happened to agree with their viewpoint on this one issue. This discussion was one of the things that led to the group becoming a lot more bureaucratic with polls and extension specs and so on. For implementors, maintaining a high cadence is extremely important, not just because it isn't anyone's job to engage i

[06:45:39.0000] <jgraham>
... debates, but also because it's necessary to keep improving specific products and the platform as a whole so as not to lose in the marketplace either against direct competitors or whole new technology stacks that don't come with all the legacy baggage. With that in mind a forum with protocols designed to neuter multi-year debates over a single attribute aren't suitable for any other kind of work, and that's why implementors lost interest.

[06:46:55.0000] <darobin>

The fact that everyone â€” in some particularly unhelpful way â€” got what they wanted, with a WHATWG spec not containing longdesc and a W3C extension spec containing longdesc, makes it unsurprising that people moved on to new things in the meantime. [08:23:29.0000] <annevk>

[08:23:58.0000] <smaug____>

[08:24:06.0000] <Domenic>

[08:24:16.0000] <Domenic>

[08:24:54.0000] <annevk>

[16:49:37.0000] <SimonSapin>
annevk: did you know that DoCoMo, KDDI, and SoftBank each have their own variant of Shift-JIS with different mappings for Emoji? What does the Encoding spec do with these?


2014-09-25
[19:14:00.0000] <GPHemsley>
SimonSapin: Maps everything to the sad-face emoticon

[20:21:07.0000] <MikeSmith>
SimonSapin: for those they're all using code points in the "user-defined" ranges of Shift-JIS

[20:22:22.0000] <MikeSmith>
SimonSapin: that is, the ranges in Shift-JIS that were intentionally set aside for private use, and not for interchange

[20:26:14.0000] <MikeSmith>
SimonSapin: so if the Encoding spec already says anything specific about how to process Shift-JIS code points in those "user defined" ranges, then I'd think the spec already has them covered

[20:27:49.0000] <MikeSmith>
SimonSapin: I wouldn't think the Encoding spec should do anything much beyond that -- certainly it shouldn't specify that they must be mapped to something else

[20:29:03.0000] <MikeSmith>
SimonSapin: because, e.g., UAs running within some system that consumes them need for them to stay just as what that system expects

[20:30:36.0000] <MikeSmith>
SimonSapin: e.g., at least for e-mail, some user sending a message within a Docomo network to another Docomo user needs for them to not be changed in between

[20:32:23.0000] <MikeSmith>
SimonSapin: it's a different thing of course for Web documents -- but authors should not be using those Shift-JIS-variant emoji in Shift-JIS Web documents anyway -- because they already can't expect any kind of interchange if they do

[20:37:25.0000] <MikeSmith>
SimonSapin: anyway, I think that at this point attempting to change any of the Web UA handling of those characters will just risk breaking existing (legacy) content
